import math

import torch
import torch.nn as nn


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Causal grouped-query attention with KV-head expansion.

    Query heads outnumber KV heads. Keys and values are expanded by repeating
    each KV head across its assigned query-head group before causal attention.
    """

    def __init__(self):
        super().__init__()

    def forward(
        self,
        q: torch.Tensor,
        k: torch.Tensor,
        v: torch.Tensor,
    ) -> torch.Tensor:
        batch_size, seq_len, num_q_heads, head_dim = q.shape
        num_kv_heads = k.shape[2]
        group_size = num_q_heads // num_kv_heads

        expanded_k = k.repeat_interleave(group_size, dim=2)
        expanded_v = v.repeat_interleave(group_size, dim=2)

        out = torch.zeros_like(q)
        scale = 1.0 / math.sqrt(head_dim)

        for b in range(batch_size):
            for h in range(num_q_heads):
                for t in range(seq_len):
                    q_t = q[b, t, h]
                    k_hist = expanded_k[b, : t + 1, h]
                    v_hist = expanded_v[b, : t + 1, h]
                    scores = (k_hist * q_t.unsqueeze(0)).sum(dim=-1) * scale
                    attn = torch.softmax(scores, dim=0)
                    out[b, t, h] = (attn.unsqueeze(-1) * v_hist).sum(dim=0)

        return out


batch_size = 2
seq_len = 80
num_q_heads = 8
num_kv_heads = 2
head_dim = 32


def get_inputs():
    q = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, num_q_heads, head_dim, dtype=torch.float32)
    k = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, num_kv_heads, head_dim, dtype=torch.float32)
    v = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, num_kv_heads, head_dim, dtype=torch.float32)
    return [q, k, v]


def get_init_inputs():
    return []

It computes attention per query position t only over past keys up to t+1 (causal). It uses GQA where kv heads count = num_q_heads // group_size, expanded by repeat_interleave along head dimension. Need a kernel that efficiently handles these small dimensions, maybe using shared memory and loop fusion. Need to produce a Python file with ModelNew class and inline CUDA loaded via torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline. Goal: speedup vs reference. Since small dims, overhead matters. Let me just write a clean CUDA kernel with one thread per (b, h, t): ```cuda __global__ void gqa_causal_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_q_heads, int num_kv_heads, int head_dim ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = batch_size * num_q_heads * seq_len; if (idx >= total) return; int t = idx % seq_len; int h = (idx / seq_len) % num_q_heads; int b = idx / (seq_len * num_q_heads); int group_size = num_q_heads / num_kv_heads; int h_kv = h / group_size; float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim); // Compute scores float scores[80]; // seq_len max is 80 float max_score = -INFINITY; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { float q_val = q[((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + d]; float k_val = k[((b * seq_len + s) * num_kv_heads + h_kv) * head_dim + d]; dot += q_val * k_val; } scores[s] = dot * scale; if (scores[s] > max_score) max_score = scores[s]; } // Softmax float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { scores[s] = expf(scores[s] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[s]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { scores[s] *= inv_sum; } // Weighted sum for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { float out_val = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { float v_val = v[((b * seq_len + s) * num_kv_heads + h_kv) * head_dim + d]; out_val += scores[s] * v_val; } out[((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + d] = out_val; } } ``` Wait, but `scores[80]` is a VLA/stack array. After computing all softmax weights, each thread loops over s and accumulates scores[s] * v[s][thread_d]. Actually, that's a nice approach! Let me detail it: Block config: 32 threads (1 warp) per block, blockIdx = (b, h, t) Thread i: handles dimension d = i For causal attention at (b, h, t): 1. Load q_d = q[b, t, h, d] into register 2. For s = 0 to t: a. Load k_d = k[b, s, h_kv, d] b. Compute prod = q_d * k_d c. Warp-level reduce prod to get dot product d. Thread 0 stores/computes score = dot * scale e. Thread 0 computes softmax in its registers (or we broadcast and all threads help) 3. Softmax normalization 4. For s = 0 to t: a. Broadcast score[s] to all threads b. Load v_d = v[b, s, h_kv, d] c. out_d += score[s] * v_d 5. Write out[b, t, h, d] = out_d Steps 2c and step 4a need warp-level communication. For step 2c, we can use warp shuffle reduction. For step 4a, __shfl_sync to broadcast from thread 0 to all threads. Actually, for step 4a, only thread 0 computed the softmax, so it needs to broadcast each score to other threads. Let me refine the kernel: ```cuda #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __forceinline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); return val; } __forceinline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_max(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val = fmaxf(val, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset)); return val; } __global__ void gqa_causal_attention( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_q_heads, int num_kv_heads, int head_dim ) { // Each block handles one (b, h, t) int t = blockIdx.x % seq_len; int h = (blockIdx.x / seq_len) % num_q_heads; int b = blockIdx.x / (seq_len * num_q_heads); int group_size = num_q_heads / num_kv_heads; int h_kv = h / group_size; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (tid >= head_dim) return; // safety, though we launch with 32 threads float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim); // Load q once int q_idx = ((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + tid; float q_val = q[q_idx]; // Compute scores float scores[80]; // t can be up to seq_len-1 = 79 // First pass: compute dot products and find max float max_score = -1e30f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { int k_idx = ((b * seq_len + s) * num_kv_heads + h_kv) * head_dim + tid; float k_val = k[k_idx]; float prod = q_val * k_val; float dot = warp_reduce_sum(prod); float score = dot * scale; // Only thread 0 has the correct dot, broadcast to all score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); scores[s] = score; if (score > max_score) max_score = score; } // Broadcast max_score max_score = warp_reduce_max(max_score); max_score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, max_score, 0); // Second pass: compute exp and sum float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { scores[s] = expf(scores[s] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[s]; } // Reduce sum_exp sum_exp = warp_reduce_sum(sum_exp); sum_exp = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, sum_exp, 0); float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { scores[s] *= inv_sum; } // Third pass: weighted sum of v float out_val = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { int v_idx = ((b * seq_len + s) * num_kv_heads + h_kv) * head_dim + tid; float v_val = v[v_idx]; out_val += scores[s] * v_val; } // Write output int out_idx = ((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + tid; out[out_idx] = out_val; } ``` Wait, this has a subtle issue. In the first pass, `max_score` is a thread-local variable. Each thread computes its own max among the scores. Then `warp_reduce_max(max_score)` finds the max across all threads. But since each thread sees the SAME scores (they were broadcast via shuffle), every thread has the same max_score! So `warp_reduce_max` is redundant but harmless. Actually wait, every thread does compute the same scores because `score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0)` broadcast the result from thread 0. This means I can simplify: - Thread 0 does the dot and stores the score, then broadcasts it - All threads receive the same score - All threads maintain identical scores array, compute same max, same sum This is fine. But if we processed multiple t values per block, they could share k and v in shared memory. For example, a block handling 8 consecutive t values (say t=0..7) for fixed (b, h) would need to read k[b, 0..7, h_kv, :] and v[b, 0..7, h_kv, :]. Each t only needs k/v up to its own t. So t=7 needs k/v up to s=7. The total k/v needed is 8*32 = 256 floats each = 1KB each. This easily fits in shared memory. But the real saving would be for larger t ranges. If a block handles t=0..31, t=31 needs k/v up to s=31. Total k/v = 32*32 = 1024 floats = 4KB each. Still fits in shared memory (228KB per SM). Actually, the key insight is: for consecutive t values with the same (b, h), they share the same k/v prefix. Block-level caching could reduce HBM traffic. However, this requires significant restructuring of the kernel. Let me see if it's worth it. If each block handles, say, 16 t values for one (b, h): - Threads: 16 warps = 512 threads, or we could use 256 threads with 16 t values and each thread doing 2 dims or using serial processing per t. - Actually, if we use 32 threads per t, 16 t values = 512 threads per block. That's allowed (max 1024). - Or we could process t values serially within each warp, using 32 threads for 1 t at a time, looping 16 times. Wait, if we process multiple t values serially in a block, we can load k and v incrementally: - For t=0: load k[0], compute score, load v[0], compute output - For t=1: load k[1], compute score with k[0..1], load v[1], update output - etc. But this is actually the same memory access pattern as our current kernel! Each t loads its own k[t] and v[t] for the score/output computation. The only difference is that multiple t computations happen in the same block, potentially allowing k and v to stay in shared memory. If we put k and v in shared memory for the block: - Process t values t_start to t_end - Load all k[b, t_start:t_end, h_kv, :] into shared memory - But each t also needs k[b, 0:t_start, h_kv, :] which was loaded by previous iterations or other blocks This gets complicated. For a small problem, I'll stick with the simpler approach. Another optimization: use `__ldg` (load through read-only cache) for q, k, v. Since we don't modify them, this can help. Each thread handles 1 dim for 1 query head. Total blocks = batch_size * group_size * seq_len = 2 * 2 * 80 = 320 blocks. Wait: num_kv_heads = 2, group_size = 4. For each (b, h_kv, t), there are 4 query heads. If one block handles all 4 query heads for one (b, h_kv, t): - 128 threads (4 warps) - Each warp handles 1 query head - k and v are loaded once and broadcast to all warps via shared memory - This saves HBM bandwidth by 4x for k and v! But we save HBM bandwidth for k and v by 4x. This seems like a great optimization! Let me think about how to implement it. Actually, to be safe, I should load k and v lazily into shared memory. For each s=0..t: 1. Warp 0 loads k[s, dim] into shared memory (all 32 dims) 2. If warp 0 loads k[s, dim] for all dims, that's already what happens naturally with coalesced access. But to share across warps, we need a barrier. Actually, here's a better approach: for each s, all warps load k[s, dim] from HBM. Each warp loads its own copy. Since there are 4 warps, k[s, :] is loaded 4 times from HBM. That's 4x the traffic we want. To avoid this, we can have warp 0 (or thread 0..31) load k[s, :] into shared memory, then __syncthreads(), then all threads read from shared memory. In the first pass (score computation): For each s: - Threads 0..31 load k[b, s, h_kv, 0..31] into smem_k[0..31] - __syncthreads() - Each warp computes q_head[h] * smem_k for its own head - Thread 0 of each warp writes the score to smem_scores[warp_id][s] - Continue to next s For softmax: - Each warp softmaxes its own scores (stored in registers or smem) - This is independent per warp For weighted sum: For each s: - Threads 0..31 load v[b, s, h_kv, 0..31] into smem_v[0..31] - __syncthreads() - Each warp computes weighted sum for its own head using smem_v and its own scores This is great! Let me write this optimized version: ```cuda __global__ void gqa_causal_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_q_heads, int num_kv_heads, int head_dim ) { int b = blockIdx.x / (num_kv_heads * seq_len); int h_kv = (blockIdx.x / seq_len) % num_kv_heads; int t = blockIdx.x % seq_len; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / head_dim; // 0..3 int dim = threadIdx.x % head_dim; // 0..31 int group_size = num_q_heads / num_kv_heads; int h = h_kv * group_size + warp_id; int total_threads = blockDim.x; __shared__ float smem_k[32]; __shared__ float smem_v[32]; __shared__ float smem_scores[4][80]; // scores for 4 warps, up to 80 time steps float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim); // Load q for this warp's head int q_idx = ((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + dim; float q_val = q[q_idx]; // Compute scores float max_score = -1e30f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { // Load k into shared memory (only first warp's threads participate) if (threadIdx.x < head_dim) { int k_idx = ((b * seq_len + s) * num_kv_heads + h_kv) * head_dim + threadIdx.x; smem_k[threadIdx.x] = k[k_idx]; } __syncthreads(); float k_val = smem_k[dim]; float prod = q_val * k_val; float dot = warp_reduce_sum(prod); float score = dot * scale; score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); if (dim == 0) { smem_scores[warp_id][s] = score; } if (score > max_score) max_score = score; } // Softmax for this warp's scores max_score = warp_reduce_max(max_score); max_score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, max_score, 0); float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { float score; if (dim == 0) score = smem_scores[warp_id][s]; score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); float exp_score = expf(score - max_score); if (dim == 0) smem_scores[warp_id][s] = exp_score; sum_exp += exp_score; } sum_exp = warp_reduce_sum(sum_exp); sum_exp = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, sum_exp, 0); float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; // Weighted sum of v float out_val = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { if (threadIdx.x < head_dim) { int v_idx = ((b * seq_len + s) * num_kv_heads + h_kv) * head_dim + threadIdx.x; smem_v[threadIdx.x] = v[v_idx]; } __syncthreads(); float score; if (dim == 0) score = smem_scores[warp_id][s]; score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); score *= inv_sum; float v_val = smem_v[dim]; out_val += score * v_val; } // Write output int out_idx = ((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + dim; out[out_idx] = out_val; } ``` Wait, I see a potential issue. I just realized about `__syncthreads()` in the presence of warp divergence. If all threads in a block reach `__syncthreads()`, it's fine. In our kernel, there are no divergent paths that skip `__syncthreads()`. All threads go through the same loops with different data. So it's safe. However, there is one small issue: in the k/v loading, we do: ```cuda if (threadIdx.x < 32) { ... } __syncthreads(); ``` On older GPUs, if some threads take a divergent path and don't reach __syncthreads(), it causes deadlock. But in this case, threads 32..127 skip the if body but still reach __syncthreads(). All threads in the block reach the __syncthreads() call, so it's fine. OK, I'm satisfied with the kernel design. Let me write a version that recomputes the scores: ```cuda __global__ void gqa_causal_attention_kernel(...) { // Setup same as before... float q_val = q[...]; float scale = ...; // First pass: compute max score float max_score = -1e30f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { // Load k into shared memory if (threadIdx.x < 32) smem_kv[threadIdx.x] = k[...]; __syncthreads(); float prod = q_val * smem_kv[lane]; float dot = warp_reduce_sum(prod); float score = dot * scale; score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); if (score > max_score) max_score = score; } max_score = warp_reduce_max(max_score); max_score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, max_score, 0); // Second pass: compute sum_exp float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { // Recompute score if (threadIdx.x < 32) smem_kv[threadIdx.x] = k[...]; __syncthreads(); float prod = q_val * smem_kv[lane]; float dot = warp_reduce_sum(prod); float score = dot * scale; score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); sum_exp += expf(score - max_score); } sum_exp = warp_reduce_sum(sum_exp); sum_exp = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, sum_exp, 0); // Third pass: weighted sum (recompute score again) float out_val = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { if (threadIdx.x < 32) smem_kv[threadIdx.x] = k[...]; __syncthreads(); float prod = q_val * smem_kv[lane]; float dot = warp_reduce_sum(prod); float score = dot * scale; score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); float weight = expf(score - max_score) / sum_exp; if (threadIdx.x < 32) smem_v[threadIdx.x] = v[...]; __syncthreads(); out_val += weight * smem_v[lane]; } // Write output out[...] = out_val; } ``` Hmm, this recomputes the dot product 3 times per s. OK, final kernel design: ```cuda __global__ void gqa_causal_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_q_heads, int num_kv_heads, int head_dim ) { int b = blockIdx.x / (num_kv_heads * seq_len); int h_kv = (blockIdx.x / seq_len) % num_kv_heads; int t = blockIdx.x % seq_len; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int group_size = num_q_heads / num_kv_heads; int h = h_kv * group_size + warp_id; __shared__ float smem_kv[32]; __shared__ float smem_scores[4][128]; float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim); float q_val = q[((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + lane]; float max_score = -1e30f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { if (threadIdx.x < 32) { smem_kv[threadIdx.x] = k[((b * seq_len + s) * num_kv_heads + h_kv) * head_dim + threadIdx.x]; } __syncthreads(); float dot = warp_reduce_sum(q_val * smem_kv[lane]); float score = dot * scale; score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); if (lane == 0) smem_scores[warp_id][s] = score; if (score > max_score) max_score = score; } max_score = warp_reduce_max(max_score); max_score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, max_score, 0); float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { float score = (lane == 0) ? smem_scores[warp_id][s] : 0.0f; score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); float e = expf(score - max_score); if (lane == 0) smem_scores[warp_id][s] = e; sum_exp += e; } sum_exp = warp_reduce_sum(sum_exp); sum_exp = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, sum_exp, 0); float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; float out_val = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { if (threadIdx.x < 32) { smem_kv[threadIdx.x] = v[((b * seq_len + s) * num_kv_heads + h_kv) * head_dim + threadIdx.x]; } __syncthreads(); float weight = (lane == 0) ? smem_scores[warp_id][s] : 0.0f; weight = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, weight, 0); weight *= inv_sum; out_val += weight * smem_kv[lane]; } out[((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + lane] = out_val; } ``` Wait, there's a potential issue with `smem_scores[4][128]`. Let me write it out. ```cuda __global__ void gqa_causal_attention_kernel(...) { extern __shared__ float smem[]; // block = (b, h_kv, t) int b = ...; int h_kv = ...; int t = ...; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int group_size = num_q_heads / num_kv_heads; int h = h_kv * group_size + warp_id; // Load k[0..t] into shared memory int k_prefix_size = (t + 1) * head_dim; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < k_prefix_size; idx += blockDim.x) { int s = idx / head_dim; int d = idx % head_dim; smem[idx] = k[((b * seq_len + s) * num_kv_heads + h_kv) * head_dim + d]; } __syncthreads(); float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim); float q_val = q[((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + lane]; // First pass: max score float max_score = -1e30f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { float dot = warp_reduce_sum(q_val * smem[s * head_dim + lane]); float score = dot * scale; score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); if (score > max_score) max_score = score; } max_score = warp_reduce_max(max_score); max_score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, max_score, 0); // Second pass: sum_exp float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { float dot = warp_reduce_sum(q_val * smem[s * head_dim + lane]); float score = dot * scale; score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); sum_exp += expf(score - max_score); } sum_exp = warp_reduce_sum(sum_exp); sum_exp = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, sum_exp, 0); float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; // Third pass: weighted sum of v float out_val = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { // Compute weight float dot = warp_reduce_sum(q_val * smem[s * head_dim + lane]); float score = dot * scale; score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); float weight = expf(score - max_score) * inv_sum; // Load v[s] float v_val = v[((b * seq_len + s) * num_kv_heads + h_kv) * head_dim + lane]; out_val += weight * v_val; } out[((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + lane] = out_val; } ``` Ooh, this is elegant! Revised smem layout: ```cuda extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_prefix = smem; float* v_buffer = smem + seq_len * head_dim; // after k_prefix reserved space ``` Shared memory size at launch: `(seq_len * head_dim + head_dim) * sizeof(float)` In the first pass, load all k[0..t] into k_prefix: ```cuda for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < (t+1) * head_dim; idx += blockDim.x) { int s = idx / head_dim; int d = idx % head_dim; k_prefix[idx] = k[...]; } __syncthreads(); ``` In the third pass: ```cuda for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { // Load v[s] into v_buffer if (threadIdx.x < 32) { v_buffer[threadIdx.x] = v[((b * seq_len + s) * num_kv_heads + h_kv) * head_dim + threadIdx.x]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute weight using k_prefix[s] float dot = warp_reduce_sum(q_val * k_prefix[s * head_dim + lane]); ... float weight = ...; out_val += weight * v_buffer[lane]; __syncthreads(); // before next iteration } ``` Wait, there's a __syncthreads() before using v_buffer and after computing weight, plus another at the start of the next iteration's load. Actually, after computing weight, different warps are still reading v_buffer in the `out_val += weight * v_buffer[lane]` line. We need a __syncthreads() after that before overwriting v_buffer in the next iteration. But do different warps read v_buffer[lane] simultaneously? Yes, all warps in the block read v_buffer at the same time (they're in lockstep within each warp). Since v_buffer is in shared memory and multiple warps read it, it's a broadcast read, which is fast. And we need __syncthreads() before the `if (threadIdx.x < 32) v_buffer[...] = ...` line to ensure all warps have finished reading v_buffer from the previous iteration. So: ```cuda for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { if (threadIdx.x < 32) { v_buffer[threadIdx.x] = v[...]; } __syncthreads(); // compute weight... out_val += weight * v_buffer[lane]; __syncthreads(); // ensure all reads done before next load } ``` Good. """ # C++ wrapper cpp_source = """ torch::Tensor gqa_causal_attention(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) { ... } """ # Compile at module load gqa_module = load_inline( name="gqa_attention", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["gqa_causal_attention"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self): super().__init__() def forward(self, q, k, v): if not q.is_cuda: q = q.cuda() k = k.cuda() v = v.cuda() move_back = True else: move_back = False out = gqa_module.gqa_causal_attention(q, k, v) if move_back: out = out.cpu() return out ``` Hmm, but if q, k, v are already on CUDA but on different devices, this could be an issue. One more optimization thought: since q is loaded once per warp, and the q tensor is (B, T, H_q, D), we access `q[((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + lane]`. This assumes contiguous layout in D-major order. For (B, T, H, D) with default PyTorch layout, the strides are (T*H*D, H*D, D, 1). So our indexing is correct for contiguous tensors. For k and v, the strides are (T*H_kv*D, H_kv*D, D, 1). Our indexing `((b * seq_len + s) * num_kv_heads + h_kv) * head_dim + lane` is correct. Good. Let me double-check the math for the index computation in the C++ wrapper when using `.contiguous()`. For a contiguous tensor of shape (B, T, H, D): - stride_B = T * H * D - stride_T = H * D - stride_H = D - stride_D = 1 Index(b, t, h, d) = b * T*H*D + t * H*D + h * D + d = ((b * T + t) * H + h) * D + d This matches our formula. Good. OK, let me write the full Python module. Wait, I realize I want to refine the kernel slightly. Currently I have: ```cuda extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_prefix = smem; float* v_buffer = smem + seq_len * head_dim; ``` For very large seq_len, this might over-allocate. But for this problem, seq_len=80. Let me make sure the dynamic shared memory request is correct. At kernel launch: ```cpp size_t smem_size = (seq_len * head_dim + head_dim) * sizeof(float); gqa_causal_attention_kernel<<<blocks, threads, smem_size>>>(...); ``` In the kernel, `smem + seq_len * head_dim` points past the k_prefix area. The total allocated is `seq_len * head_dim + head_dim` floats. So v_buffer has `head_dim` elements. Good. But wait, `smem` is an array of `float`. In CUDA, `extern __shared__ float smem[];` declares a dynamically-sized shared memory array. The size is given in the third argument to `<<< >>>`. One thing to note: if there are any other statically-declared `__shared__` variables in the kernel, they would occupy the start of shared memory. But in our kernel, all shared memory is accessed through `smem`. There are no `__shared__ float arr[...]` declarations. Good. Wait, actually there is a subtle issue. Multiple `extern __shared__` arrays with different types might overlap. But we only have one. Another thing: in the k prefix loading loop: ```cuda for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < (t + 1) * head_dim; idx += blockDim.x) { int s = idx / head_dim; int d = idx % head_dim; k_prefix[idx] = k[((b * seq_len + s) * num_kv_heads + h_kv) * head_dim + d]; } __syncthreads(); ``` This is coalesced because threads with consecutive `threadIdx.x` access consecutive elements of `k`. For example, thread 0 accesses k[..., 0], thread 1 accesses k[..., 1], etc. Since the last dimension is D with stride 1, this is perfectly coalesced. Similarly for the v loading. But note: when different blocks process different t values, they access different ranges of k. For example, block (b, h_kv, t=0) accesses k[b, 0, h_kv, :]. Block (b, h_kv, t=80) accesses k[b, 0..80, h_kv, :]. Since blocks run concurrently in arbitrary order, there's no guaranteed sequential access pattern. But L2 cache will still capture locality when blocks for the same (b, h_kv) run on the same SM or nearby SMs. One potential issue: in the score computation loop, we do: ```cuda for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { float dot = warp_reduce_sum(q_val * k_prefix[s * head_dim + lane]); ... } ``` All 32 threads in a warp read `k_prefix[s * head_dim + lane]` where lane goes from 0 to 31. This is a contiguous 32-float read from shared memory. In shared memory, consecutive addresses map to different banks (assuming 32 banks, each float goes to a different bank). So there's no bank conflict - each thread accesses a different bank. This is optimal! For the v_buffer access in the third pass, same thing: `v_buffer[lane]` has no bank conflicts. What about multiple warps accessing shared memory simultaneously? - In the first pass, all 4 warps read `k_prefix[s * head_dim + lane]` at the same time. Since each warp reads the same address range (banks 0..31), this is a broadcast to each warp, which is handled efficiently. - In the third pass, same thing with v_buffer. This is great. One final thing: I should make sure to use `__restrict__` on all pointers to help the compiler optimize. Also, `-O3` and maybe `-use_fast_math` for faster expf? `-use_fast_math` makes `expf` faster but less accurate. PyTorch uses a high-quality exp implementation. For tolerance 1e-4, `-use_fast_math` should still pass. But let me start without it and see. Actually, I want to make sure that `expf` with `-O3` matches PyTorch's `torch.exp` within 1e-4. PyTorch typically uses the same underlying CUDA exp as `expf` in the standard math library. For fp32, the maximum error of `expf` is typically < 1 ulp, which is much smaller than 1e-4. So it should be fine. Let me now write the actual code. ```python import math import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r""" #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __forceinline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); return val; } __forceinline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_max(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val = fmaxf(val, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset)); return val; } __global__ void gqa_causal_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_q_heads, int num_kv_heads, int head_dim ) { // Block mapping: one block per (batch, kv_head, time) int t = blockIdx.x % seq_len; int h_kv = (blockIdx.x / seq_len) % num_kv_heads; int b = blockIdx.x / (seq_len * num_kv_heads); int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int group_size = num_q_heads / num_kv_heads; int h = h_kv * group_size + warp_id; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_prefix = smem; float* v_buffer = smem + seq_len * head_dim; // Load k prefix [0..t] into shared memory int prefix_size = (t + 1) * head_dim; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < prefix_size; idx += blockDim.x) { int s = idx / head_dim; int d = idx % head_dim; k_prefix[idx] = k[((b * seq_len + s) * num_kv_heads + h_kv) * head_dim + d]; } __syncthreads(); float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim); float q_val = q[((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + lane]; // First pass: compute max score float max_score = -1e30f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { float prod = q_val * k_prefix[s * head_dim + lane]; float dot = warp_reduce_sum(prod); float score = dot * scale; score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); if (score > max_score) max_score = score; } max_score = warp_reduce_max(max_score); max_score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, max_score, 0); // Second pass: compute sum of exp scores float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { float prod = q_val * k_prefix[s * head_dim + lane]; float dot = warp_reduce_sum(prod); float score = dot * scale; score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); sum_exp += expf(score - max_score); } sum_exp = warp_reduce_sum(sum_exp); sum_exp = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, sum_exp, 0); float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; // Third pass: compute weighted sum of v float out_val = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { // Load v[s] into shared memory if (threadIdx.x < 32) { v_buffer[threadIdx.x] = v[((b * seq_len + s) * num_kv_heads + h_kv) * head_dim + threadIdx.x]; } __syncthreads(); float prod = q_val * k_prefix[s * head_dim + lane]; float dot = warp_reduce_sum(prod); float score = dot * scale; score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); float weight = expf(score - max_score) * inv_sum; out_val += weight * v_buffer[lane]; __syncthreads(); } out[((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + lane] = out_val; } """ CPP_SOURCE = r""" torch::Tensor gqa_causal_attention(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) { int batch_size = q.size(0); int seq_len = q.size(1); int num_q_heads = q.size(2); int head_dim = q.size(3); int num_kv_heads = k.size(2); int group_size = num_q_heads / num_kv_heads; int threads_per_block = group_size * head_dim; int total_blocks = batch_size * num_kv_heads * seq_len; size_t smem_size = (seq_len * head_dim + head_dim) * sizeof(float); auto out = torch::zeros_like(q); gqa_causal_attention_kernel<<<total_blocks, threads_per_block, smem_size>>>( q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, seq_len, num_q_heads, num_kv_heads, head_dim ); return out; } """ gqa_cuda = load_inline( name="gqa_attention", cpp_sources=CPP_SOURCE, cuda_sources=CUDA_SOURCE, functions=["gqa_causal_attention"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self): super().__init__() def forward(self, q, k, v): q = q.contiguous() k = k.contiguous() v = v.contiguous() if not q.is_cuda: q = q.cuda() k = k.cuda() v = v.cuda() move_back = True else: move_back = False out = gqa_cuda.gqa_causal_attention(q, k, v) if move_back: out = out.cpu() return out ``` Wait, I'm worried about one thing. In the C++ wrapper, I pass `q.data_ptr<float>()`. But if q is on CPU while the kernel expects GPU pointers, this will crash. My `if not q.is_cuda` check moves q to cuda before calling the kernel, so it should be fine. But what if the module is loaded in an environment without CUDA? `load_inline` would fail at module load time because it tries to compile CUDA code. Since this is a GPU kernel challenge, I assume CUDA is available. Actually, I'm realizing there might be an issue with `load_inline` running at module import time. If I'm constantly iterating on the kernel, I need to make sure the module reloads each time. In the evaluation, exec() is called once, so module import happens once. That's fine. One more thought: in `CPP_SOURCE`, `gqa_causal_attention_kernel` is defined in `CUDA_SOURCE`, so it should be visible in the C++ source. With `load_inline`, the CUDA and C++ sources are combined into one file for compilation. So the kernel declaration is available. Good. Actually, I'll add a forward declaration in the C++ source just to be safe, though it's not strictly necessary: ```cpp void gqa_causal_attention_kernel(...); ``` Wait, the kernel has `__global__` keyword, so in C++ host code we can't call it directly with `kernel<<<...>>>` unless it's declared. In NVCC, a `__global__` function defined before the host code can be called. But since the C++ source and CUDA source are concatenated by `load_inline`, I need to make sure the order is correct. `load_inline` puts `cuda_sources` before `cpp_sources` in the combined file. So the kernel is defined before the C++ wrapper calls it. Good. Wait, I just recalled: `load_inline` auto-generates the pybind11 bindings from the `functions` list. So I don't need to write any PYBIND11_MODULE code. Good. Let me think about whether there are any numerical issues with `expf`. For scores that are large negative numbers after subtracting max, `expf` gives very small values. For scores near max, `expf(0) = 1`. This should match PyTorch. One potential issue: if all scores are very similar, `score - max_score` might be a small negative number. `expf` of small negative numbers should be accurate. But what if `max_score` is infinity or NaN? The reference would also have issues in that case, and for normal random inputs, this shouldn't happen. For t=0 (first timestep), `max_score` is just the score for s=0. `sum_exp = expf(0) = 1`. `inv_sum = 1`. The output is `q[0]·k[0] * scale / 1 * v[0]`. Wait, no: `weight = expf(0) * 1 = 1`. `out_val = 1 * v[0]`. That's correct for causal attention at t=0. Wait, score = dot * scale. So for t=0, the attention always outputs v[0] regardless of the q·k similarity, because there's only one key. That's exactly what causal attention does. For t=1, score_0 and score_1. Let me rewrite with score storage: ```cuda extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_prefix = smem; float* scores_smem = smem + seq_len * head_dim; // group_size = blockDim.x / 32 float* v_buffer = smem + seq_len * head_dim + seq_len * (blockDim.x / 32); // First pass: compute scores and max float max_score = -1e30f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { float prod = q_val * k_prefix[s * head_dim + lane]; float dot = warp_reduce_sum(prod); float score = dot * scale; score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); if (lane == 0) { scores_smem[warp_id * seq_len + s] = score; } if (score > max_score) max_score = score; } __syncthreads(); // wait for all scores to be written // Softmax: compute exp and sum max_score = warp_reduce_max(max_score); max_score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, max_score, 0); float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { float score = (lane == 0) ? scores_smem[warp_id * seq_len + s] : 0.0f; score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); float e = expf(score - max_score); if (lane == 0) scores_smem[warp_id * seq_len + s] = e; sum_exp += e; } __syncthreads(); sum_exp = warp_reduce_sum(sum_exp); sum_exp = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, sum_exp, 0); float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; // Weighted sum float out_val = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { if (threadIdx.x < 32) { v_buffer[threadIdx.x] = v[...]; } __syncthreads(); float weight = (lane == 0) ? scores_smem[warp_id * seq_len + s] : 0.0f; weight = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, weight, 0); weight *= inv_sum; out_val += weight * v_buffer[lane]; __syncthreads(); } ``` Wait, `blockDim.x / 32` gives the number of warps, which is `group_size`. For `seq_len=80`, row offset is 80 floats = 80 * 4 = 320 bytes. Shared memory banks are 4 bytes wide, so bank index = (address / 4) % 32. Row 0, column s: address = base + s * 4. Bank = s % 32. Row 1, column s: address = base + 320 + s * 4. Bank = (80 + s) % 32 = (16 + s) % 32. Row 2, column s: bank = (160 + s) % 32 = s % 32. Row 3, column s: bank = (240 + s) % 32 = (16 + s) % 32. When multiple warps read the exact same shared memory addresses, it causes bank conflicts because all threads in all warps want to read the same bank. On modern GPUs (Volta+), shared memory has broadcast capability where if multiple threads in a warp read the same address, it's a broadcast (1 transaction). But if multiple THREADS from DIFFERENT WARPS read the same address, it might still be a single transaction or multiple depending on the hardware. Actually, I need to check: for H100, when 4 warps each read 32 consecutive floats starting at the same address (k_prefix[s * 32 + 0..31]), is there a broadcast across warps? But technically, since each bank can only serve one request per cycle, if 4 warps all request data from the same bank, it might take 4 cycles. However, the hardware might combine these requests. But let me reconsider whether multiple warps reading the same k_prefix simultaneously is actually a problem. In modern CUDA, shared memory is partitioned per-SM. All warps on the same SM access the same shared memory. The hardware can serve multiple requests per cycle if they're to different banks. If they're to the same bank, it's serialized. For k_prefix[s * 32 + lane], lane 0 of each warp accesses bank s*32 % 32 = 0. Lanes 0..31 of warp 0 access banks 0..31. Lanes 0..31 of warp 1 also access banks 0..31! The hardware might need 4 cycles to serve all requests. At 2 GHz SM frequency, 4 cycles = 2 nanoseconds. The first pass does 80 iterations, so 80 * 2ns = 160ns for shared memory reads. This is indeed negligible compared to HBM access. For the score computation, each iteration also does a warp shuffle reduce. Warp shuffle is register-to-register and very fast. Wait, one more thing about `__syncthreads()`. In the third pass: ```cuda for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { if (threadIdx.x < 32) { v_buffer[threadIdx.x] = v[...]; } __syncthreads(); // all threads read v_buffer out_val += weight * v_buffer[lane]; __syncthreads(); // wait for all threads to finish reading before next iteration } ``` The second `__syncthreads()` ensures that all threads finish reading v_buffer before the next iteration's write. For the first pass, after the initial k_prefix load: ```cuda for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < prefix_size; idx += blockDim.x) { k_prefix[idx] = k[...]; } __syncthreads(); ``` This sync is necessary because not all threads participate in the loop equally (the loop has `idx += blockDim.x`, so threads might do different numbers of iterations depending on prefix_size). Wait, actually for a given prefix_size, all threads do either floor(prefix_size/blockDim.x) or ceil(...) iterations, but they all execute the loop condition check and potentially exit together. In CUDA, if threads diverge in a loop, the compiler ensures all threads reach __syncthreads() unless there's a return or break that some take and others don't. Our loop: ```cuda for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < prefix_size; idx += blockDim.x) { // body } __syncthreads(); ``` This is a standard parallel load pattern. All threads enter the loop, and they all exit together (eventually, when idx >= prefix_size for all threads). So __syncthreads() is safe. OK, I'm confident. Let me write the code and compile it. The next operation or `.cpu()` will synchronize. Actually, let me not add explicit synchronization. PyTorch handles it. Let me write the complete code now. ```python import math import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r""" #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __forceinline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); return val; } __forceinline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_max(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val = fmaxf(val, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset)); return val; } __global__ void gqa_causal_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_q_heads, int num_kv_heads, int head_dim ) { int t = blockIdx.x % seq_len; int h_kv = (blockIdx.x / seq_len) % num_kv_heads; int b = blockIdx.x / (seq_len * num_kv_heads); int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int group_size = num_q_heads / num_kv_heads; int h = h_kv * group_size + warp_id; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_prefix = smem; float* scores_smem = smem + seq_len * head_dim; float* v_buffer = smem + seq_len * head_dim + seq_len * group_size; // Load k[0..t] into shared memory int prefix_size = (t + 1) * head_dim; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < prefix_size; idx += blockDim.x) { int s = idx / head_dim; int d = idx % head_dim; k_prefix[idx] = k[((b * seq_len + s) * num_kv_heads + h_kv) * head_dim + d]; } __syncthreads(); float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim); float q_val = q[((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + lane]; // First pass: compute scores and find max float max_score = -1e30f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { float prod = q_val * k_prefix[s * head_dim + lane]; float dot = warp_reduce_sum(prod); float score = dot * scale; score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); if (lane == 0) { scores_smem[warp_id * seq_len + s] = score; } if (score > max_score) max_score = score; } // Softmax max_score = warp_reduce_max(max_score); max_score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, max_score, 0); float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { float score = (lane == 0) ? scores_smem[warp_id * seq_len + s] : 0.0f; score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, score, 0); float e = expf(score - max_score); if (lane == 0) { scores_smem[warp_id * seq_len + s] = e; } sum_exp += e; } sum_exp = warp_reduce_sum(sum_exp); sum_exp = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, sum_exp, 0); float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; // Third pass: weighted sum of v float out_val = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; s++) { if (threadIdx.x < 32) { v_buffer[threadIdx.x] = v[((b * seq_len + s) * num_kv_heads + h_kv) * head_dim + threadIdx.x]; } __syncthreads(); float weight = (lane == 0) ? scores_smem[warp_id * seq_len + s] : 0.0f; weight = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, weight, 0); weight *= inv_sum; out_val += weight * v_buffer[lane]; __syncthreads(); } out[((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + lane] = out_val; } """ CPP_SOURCE = r""" torch::Tensor gqa_causal_attention(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) { int batch_size = q.size(0); int seq_len = q.size(1); int num_q_heads = q.size(2); int head_dim = q.size(3); int num_kv_heads = k.size(2); int group_size = num_q_heads / num_kv_heads; auto out = torch::zeros_like(q); int threads_per_block = group_size * head_dim; int total_blocks = batch_size * num_kv_heads * seq_len; size_t smem_size = (seq_len * head_dim + seq_len * group_size + head_dim) * sizeof(float); gqa_causal_attention_kernel<<<total_blocks, threads_per_block, smem_size>>>( q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, seq_len, num_q_heads, num_kv_heads, head_dim ); return out; } """ gqa_cuda = load_inline( name="gqa_causal_attention", cpp_sources=CPP_SOURCE, cuda_sources=CUDA_SOURCE, functions=["gqa_causal_attention"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self): super().__init__() def forward(self, q, k, v): q = q.contiguous() k = k.contiguous() v = v.contiguous() if not q.is_cuda: q = q.cuda() k = k.cuda() v = v.cuda() move_back = True else: move_back = False out = gqa_cuda.gqa_causal_attention(q, k, v) if move_back: out = out.cpu() return out ``` Wait, I need to double-check: `load_inline` auto-generates pybind11 bindings. The `functions` argument lists host-side wrapper functions from the C++ source. But `gqa_causal_attention` is defined in `cpp_sources` and it's not inside a class or namespace - it's a free function. `load_inline` will wrap it with pybind11. Good. turn 3llm latency: 9.4s
reasoning
The issue is that when I use `load_inline`, the cpp_sources gets compiled into a separate .cpp file, and the cuda_sources into a separate .cu file. To make the C++ code see the CUDA kernel declaration, I need to forward-declare the kernel in the cpp source, or put everything in cpp_sources with a `.cu` extension in the string. Actually, `load_inline` supports `with_cuda=True` and compiles cuda_sources separately. The C++ code cannot call a CUDA kernel directly unless it's forward-declared. Let me check `load_inline` docs: when using it this way, if I declare `extern "C"` or forward-declare the kernel in the cpp source, the C++ compiler should be able to resolve it. Actually, a simpler approach: I can put the CUDA code into `cpp_sources` but use the `.cu` file extension in a single file, or I can forward-declare the kernel in the cpp source. Let me forward-declare the kernel in the C++ source.

But across the block, 4 warps write to 4 different h, so 4 different memory regions. Each warp's access is coalesced. The memory layout is [b, t, h, d]. For a given b and t, h varies. The stride between h=0 and h=1 is head_dim floats. So warp 0 writes at offset h*head_dim, warp 1 at offset (h+1)*head_dim, etc. Since warps execute sequentially in terms of memory transactions (they don't try to coalesce across warps), this is fine. Each warp has a perfect coalesced 128-byte write. Great. Now let's fix the build issue and the sum_exp bug. For the sum_exp bug fix: ```cpp float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s <= t; ++s) { if (lane == 0) { float score = scores_smem[warp_id * seq_len + s]; float e = expf(score - max_score); scores_smem[warp_id * seq_len + s] = e; sum_exp += e; } } sum_exp = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, sum_exp, 0); ``` For the build issue, I'll try the ctypes preload approach. Actually, we can also try to change `name` to something without "gqa" in case there's a conflict with existing modules. idx < prefix_size; idx += blockDim.x) {\n const int s = idx / head_dim;\n const int d = idx % head_dim;\n k_prefix[idx] = k[((b * seq_len + s) * num_kv_heads + hkv) * head_dim + d];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n if (h >= num_q_heads) return;\n\n const float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim);\n const float q_val = q[((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + lane];\n\n // Pass 1: compute dot products and find max score\n float max_score = -1e30f;\n for (int s = 0; This happens because the compiled .so requires a newer libstdc++ than the system default. One workaround: use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load` with `extra_cflags` that link the correct library OR avoid the C++ ABI issue entirely by making the cpp_sources properly match. Wait, actually looking at the compilation errors more carefully: 1. With cpp_sources having forward decl + wrapper - the compilation succeeds (no CXXABI error at compile time), but then at runtime there's CXXABI_1.3.15 2. The `ctypes.CDLL(..., RTLD_GLOBAL)` didn't work because the load happens at process start before the environment variable takes effect? No, it should work. Actually, wait - looking at the error again: ``` ImportError: /lib/x86_64-linux-gnu/libstdc++.so.6: version `CXXABI_1.3.15' not found ``` The path shows `/lib/x86_64-linux-gnu/libstdc++.so.6` not `/opt/conda/lib/libstdc++.so.6`. This means the runtime linker is finding the system libstdc++ first. There are a few ways to fix this: 1. `LD_LIBRARY_PATH` environment variable BEFORE python starts 2. `LD_PRELOAD` 3. Use `-Wl,-rpath` to embed the search path in the .so 4. `ctypes.CDLL` - but it needs to be loaded before import For `ctypes.CDLL` to work, I need to import and call it before `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` executes. But I tried putting it at module level before the load_inline call and it still didn't work. Wait, maybe the process doesn't have `/opt/conda/lib/libstdc++.so.6` available or maybe the .so was already loaded and cached. Another approach: since we can't easily fix the ABI issue with inline loading, maybe I should implement the whole thing using pure PyTorch operations (no CUDA C), just optimized torch kernels. But the PyTorch reference already does softmax(this) step by step with loops - maybe I can vectorize it with broadcasting and causal masking? Actually, looking at the problem more carefully: this is causal grouped query attention. I can implement it entirely with PyTorch ops: 1. Expand k and v using repeat_interleave 2. Compute q @ k^T for all timesteps, but with causal mask 3. Apply softmax 4. Multiply by v This is basically just computing standard causal attention. Let me think if PyTorch already has a fast path for this. The `torch.nn.functional.scaled_dot_product_attention` can do causal attention and might be faster. But there's a catch - the reference implementation does NOT use standard batched matrix multiplication. It uses a manual loop. The standard `scaled_dot_product_attention` expects full sequences and masks out the future.
